In 1864, Marx became involved in the [International Workingmen's Association](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Workingmen%27s_Association "International Workingmen's Association") (also known as the First International),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicolaievskyMaenchen-Helfen2007[httpswebarchiveorgweb20130622083426httpbooksgooglecombooksid4rbH49xtcpkCpgPA192_192]_125-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolaievskyMaenchen-Helfen2007[httpswebarchiveorgweb20130622083426httpbooksgooglecombooksid4rbH49xtcpkCpgPA192_192]-125">[124]</a></sup> to whose General Council he was elected at its inception in 1864.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicolaievskyMaenchen-Helfen2007[httpswebarchiveorgweb20130616235612httpbooksgooglecombooksid4rbH49xtcpkCpgPA269_267]_159-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicolaievskyMaenchen-Helfen2007[httpswebarchiveorgweb20130616235612httpbooksgooglecombooksid4rbH49xtcpkCpgPA269_267]-159">[158]</a></sup> In that organisation, Marx was involved in the struggle against the anarchist wing centred on [Mikhail Bakunin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin "Mikhail Bakunin") (1814–1876).<sup id="cite_ref-egsbio_137-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-egsbio-137">[136]</a></sup> Although Marx won this contest, the transfer of the seat of the General Council from London to New York in 1872, which Marx supported, led to the decline of the International.<sup id="cite_ref-JessopWheatley1999-526_160-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-JessopWheatley1999-526-160">[159]</a></sup> The most important political event during the existence of the International was the [Paris Commune](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paris_Commune "Paris Commune") of 1871 when the citizens of Paris rebelled against their government and held the city for two months. In response to the bloody suppression of this rebellion, Marx wrote one of his most famous pamphlets, "[The Civil War in France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Civil_War_in_France "The Civil War in France")", a defence of the Commune.<sup id="cite_ref-Curtis1997_161-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Curtis1997-161">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup>

Given the repeated failures and frustrations of workers' revolutions and movements, Marx also sought to understand and provide a critique suitable for the [capitalist mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitalist_mode_of_production_(Marxist_theory) "Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)"), and hence spent a great deal of time in the reading room of the [British Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Museum "British Museum") studying.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220_163-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220-163">[162]</a></sup> By 1857, Marx had accumulated over 800 pages of notes and short essays on capital, [landed property](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landed_property "Landed property"), wage labour, the state, and foreign trade, and the world market, though this work did not appear in print until 1939, under the title _[Outlines of the Critique of Political Economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grundrisse "Grundrisse")_.<sup id="cite_ref-Segrest2002_164-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Segrest2002-164">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>

In 1859, Marx published _[A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Contribution_to_the_Critique_of_Political_Economy "A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy")_,<sup id="cite_ref-167"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> his first serious critique of political economy. This work was intended merely as a preview of his three-volume _[Das Kapital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Das_Kapital "Das Kapital")_ (English title: _Capital: Critique of Political Economy_), which he intended to publish at a later date. In _A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy_, Marx began to critically examine axioms and categories of economic thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPostone199354–55,_173,_192_168-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPostone199354%E2%80%9355,_173,_192-168">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_170-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-:0-170">[169]</a></sup> The work was enthusiastically received, and the edition sold out quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973318_171-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973318-171">[170]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Karl-Marx.jpg/170px-Karl-Marx.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Karl-Marx.jpg)

Marx photographed by John Mayall, 1875

The successful sales of _[A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Contribution_to_the_Critique_of_Political_Economy "A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy")_ stimulated Marx in the early 1860s to finish work on the three large volumes that would compose his major life's work – _Das Kapital_ and the _[Theories of Surplus Value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theories_of_Surplus_Value "Theories of Surplus Value")_, which discussed and critiqued the theoreticians of political economy, particularly [Adam Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adam_Smith "Adam Smith") and [David Ricardo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Ricardo "David Ricardo").<sup id="cite_ref-egsbio_137-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-egsbio-137">[136]</a></sup> _Theories of Surplus Value_ is often referred to as the fourth volume of _Das Kapital_ and constitutes one of the first comprehensive treatises on the [history of economic thought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_economic_thought "History of economic thought").<sup id="cite_ref-Rockmore2002_172-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Rockmore2002-172">[171]</a></sup> In 1867, the first volume of _Das Kapital_ was published, a work which critically analysed capital.<sup id="cite_ref-BrewerMarx1984-15_173-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-BrewerMarx1984-15-173">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_170-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-:0-170">[169]</a></sup> _Das Kapital_ proposes an explanation of the "laws of motion" of the [mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_of_production "Mode of production") from its origins to its future by describing the dynamics of the accumulation of capital, with topics such as the growth of [wage labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wage_labour "Wage labour"), the transformation of the workplace, [capital accumulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capital_accumulation "Capital accumulation"), competition, the [banking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banking "Banking") system, the [tendency of the rate of profit to fall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tendency_of_the_rate_of_profit_to_fall "Tendency of the rate of profit to fall") and land-rents, as well as how waged labour continually reproduce the rule of capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPostone2006190,_26–27._135,_374–75_174-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPostone2006190,_26%E2%80%9327._135,_374%E2%80%9375-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2012_175-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2012-175">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPepperell2010104–105_176-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPepperell2010104%E2%80%93105-176">[175]</a></sup> Marx proposes that the driving force of capital is in the [exploitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploitation_of_labour "Exploitation of labour") of [labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labour_(economics) "Labour (economics)"), whose unpaid work is the ultimate source of [surplus value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surplus_value "Surplus value").

Demand for a Russian language edition of _Das Kapital_ soon led to the printing of 3,000 copies of the book in the Russian language, which was published on 27 March 1872. By the autumn of 1871, the entire first edition of the German-language edition of _Das Kapital_ had been sold out and a second edition was published.

Volumes II and III of _Das Kapital_ remained mere manuscripts upon which Marx continued to work for the rest of his life. Both volumes were published by Engels after Marx's death.<sup id="cite_ref-egsbio_137-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-egsbio-137">[136]</a></sup> Volume II of _Das Kapital_ was prepared and published by Engels in July 1893 under the name _Capital II: The Process of Circulation of Capital_.<sup id="cite_ref-177"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> Volume III of _Das Kapital_ was published a year later in October 1894 under the name _Capital III: The Process of Capitalist Production as a Whole_.<sup id="cite_ref-178"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> _Theories of Surplus Value_ derived from the sprawling _Economic Manuscripts of 1861–1863_, a _second_ draft for _Das Kapital_, the latter spanning volumes 30–34 of the _Collected Works of Marx and Engels_. Specifically, _Theories of Surplus Value_ runs from the latter part of the _Collected Works'_ thirtieth volume through the end of their thirty-second volume;<sup id="cite_ref-179"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> meanwhile, the larger _Economic Manuscripts of 1861–1863_ run from the start of the _Collected Works'_ thirtieth volume through the first half of their thirty-fourth volume. The latter half of the Collected Works' thirty-fourth volume consists of the surviving fragments of the _Economic Manuscripts of 1863–1864_, which represented a _third_ draft for _Das Kapital_, and a large portion of which is included as an appendix to the Penguin edition of _Das Kapital_, volume I.<sup id="cite_ref-182"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> A German-language abridged edition of _Theories of Surplus Value_ was published in 1905 and in 1910. This abridged edition was translated into English and published in 1951 in London, but the complete unabridged edition of _Theories of Surplus Value_ was published as the "fourth volume" of _Das Kapital_ in 1963 and 1971 in Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-183"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Marx_old.jpg/170px-Marx_old.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Marx_old.jpg)

Marx in 1882

During the last decade of his life, Marx's health declined, and he became incapable of the sustained effort that had characterised his previous work.<sup id="cite_ref-egsbio_137-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-egsbio-137">[136]</a></sup> He did manage to comment substantially on contemporary politics, particularly in Germany and Russia. His _[Critique of the Gotha Programme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme "Critique of the Gotha Programme")_ opposed the tendency of his followers [Wilhelm Liebknecht](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Liebknecht "Wilhelm Liebknecht") and [August Bebel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/August_Bebel "August Bebel") to compromise with the [state socialist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_socialism "State socialism") ideas of [Ferdinand Lassalle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_Lassalle "Ferdinand Lassalle") in the interests of a united socialist party.<sup id="cite_ref-egsbio_137-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-egsbio-137">[136]</a></sup> This work is also notable for another famous Marx quote: "[From each according to his ability, to each according to his need](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/From_each_according_to_his_ability,_to_each_according_to_his_need "From each according to his ability, to each according to his need")".<sup id="cite_ref-CGP_P1_184-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-CGP_P1-184">[183]</a></sup>

In a letter to [Vera Zasulich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vera_Zasulich "Vera Zasulich") dated 8 March 1881, Marx contemplated the possibility of Russia's bypassing the capitalist stage of development and building communism on the basis of the common ownership of land characteristic of the village _[mir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir_(social) "Mir (social)")_.<sup id="cite_ref-egsbio_137-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-egsbio-137">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> While admitting that Russia's rural "commune is the fulcrum of social regeneration in Russia", Marx also warned that in order for the mir to operate as a means for moving straight to the socialist stage without a preceding capitalist stage it "would first be necessary to eliminate the deleterious influences which are assailing it \[the rural commune\] from all sides".<sup id="cite_ref-k72_186-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-k72-186">[185]</a></sup> Given the elimination of these pernicious influences, Marx allowed that "normal conditions of spontaneous development" of the rural commune could exist.<sup id="cite_ref-k72_186-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-k72-186">[185]</a></sup> However, in the same letter to Vera Zasulich he points out that "at the core of the capitalist system ... lies the complete separation of the producer from the means of production".<sup id="cite_ref-k72_186-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-k72-186">[185]</a></sup> In one of the drafts of this letter, Marx reveals his growing passion for anthropology, motivated by his belief that future communism would be a return on a higher level to the communism of our prehistoric past. He wrote that "the historical trend of our age is the fatal crisis which capitalist production has undergone in the European and American countries where it has reached its highest peak, a crisis that will end in its destruction, in the return of modern society to a higher form of the most archaic type – collective production and appropriation". He added that "the vitality of primitive communities was incomparably greater than that of Semitic, Greek, Roman, etc. societies, and, a fortiori, that of modern capitalist societies".<sup id="cite_ref-187"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup> Before he died, Marx asked Engels to write up these ideas, which were published in 1884 under the title _[The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Origin_of_the_Family,_Private_Property_and_the_State "The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State")_.

## Personal life

### Family

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jenny_laura_marx.jpg/170px-Jenny_laura_marx.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Jenny_laura_marx.jpg)

Jenny Carolina and Jenny Laura Marx (1869): all the Marx daughters were named Jenny in honour of their mother, Jenny von Westphalen.

Marx and von Westphalen had seven children together, but partly owing to the poor conditions in which they lived whilst in London, only three survived to adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-188"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> Their children were: [Jenny Caroline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jenny_Longuet "Jenny Longuet") (m. Longuet; 1844–1883); [Jenny Laura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laura_Marx "Laura Marx") (m. Lafargue; 1845–1911); Edgar (1847–1855); Henry Edward Guy ("Guido"; 1849–1850); Jenny Eveline Frances ("Franziska"; 1851–1852); [Jenny Julia Eleanor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eleanor_Marx "Eleanor Marx") (1855–1898) and one more who died before being named (July 1857). According to his son-in-law, [Paul Lafargue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Lafargue "Paul Lafargue"), Marx was a loving father.<sup id="cite_ref-189"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> In 1962, there were allegations that Marx fathered a son, Freddy,<sup id="cite_ref-Montefiore_190-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Montefiore-190">[189]</a></sup> out of wedlock by his housekeeper, [Helene Demuth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helene_Demuth "Helene Demuth"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheen2001173_191-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheen2001173-191">[190]</a></sup> but the claim is disputed for lack of documented evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Carver,_1991_192-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Carver,_1991-192">[191]</a></sup>

Marx frequently used pseudonyms, often when renting a house or flat, apparently to make it harder for the authorities to track him down. While in Paris, he used that of "Monsieur Ramboz", whilst in London, he signed off his letters as "A. Williams". His friends referred to him as "Moor", owing to his dark complexion and black curly hair, while he encouraged his children to call him "Old Nick" and "Charley".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheen2001152_193-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheen2001152-193">[192]</a></sup> He also bestowed nicknames and pseudonyms on his friends and family, referring to Friedrich Engels as "General", his housekeeper Helene as "Lenchen" or "Nym", while one of his daughters, Jennychen, was referred to as "Qui Qui, [Emperor of China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emperor_of_China "Emperor of China")" and another, Laura, was known as "[Kakadou](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cockatoo "Cockatoo")" or "the [Hottentot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khoikhoi "Khoikhoi")".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheen2001152_193-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheen2001152-193">[192]</a></sup>

### Health

Marx drank heavily after joining the Trier Tavern Club drinking society in the 1830s, and continued to do so until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-drinking1_31-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-drinking1-31">[30]</a></sup>

Marx was afflicted by poor health (what he himself described as "the wretchedness of existence")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg200098_194-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg200098-194">[193]</a></sup> and various authors have sought to describe and explain it. His biographer Werner Blumenberg attributed it to liver and gall problems which Marx had in 1849 and from which he was never afterward free, exacerbated by an unsuitable lifestyle. The attacks often came with headaches, eye inflammation, neuralgia in the head, and rheumatic pains. A serious nervous disorder appeared in 1877 and protracted insomnia was a consequence, which Marx fought with narcotics. The illness was aggravated by excessive nocturnal work and faulty diet. Marx was fond of highly seasoned dishes, smoked fish, caviare, pickled cucumbers, "none of which are good for liver patients", but he also liked wine and liqueurs and smoked an enormous amount "and since he had no money, it was usually bad-quality cigars". From 1863, Marx complained a lot about boils: "These are very frequent with liver patients and may be due to the same causes".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg2000100_195-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg2000100-195">[194]</a></sup> The abscesses were so bad that Marx could neither sit nor work upright. According to Blumenberg, Marx's irritability is often found in liver patients:

> The illness emphasised certain traits in his character. He argued cuttingly, his biting satire did not shrink at insults, and his expressions could be rude and cruel. Though in general Marx had blind faith in his closest friends, nevertheless he himself complained that he was sometimes too mistrustful and unjust even to them. His verdicts, not only about enemies but even about friends, were sometimes so harsh that even less sensitive people would take offence ... There must have been few whom he did not criticize like this ... not even Engels was an exception.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg200099–100_196-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg200099%E2%80%93100-196">[195]</a></sup>

According to Princeton historian [Jerrold Seigel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerrold_Seigel "Jerrold Seigel"), in his late teens, Marx may have had pneumonia or pleurisy, the effects of which led to his being exempted from Prussian military service. In later life whilst working on _Das Kapital_ (which he never completed),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg200098_194-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlumenberg200098-194">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeigel1978494_197-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeigel1978494-197">[196]</a></sup> Marx suffered from a trio of afflictions. A liver ailment, probably hereditary, was aggravated by overwork, a bad diet, and lack of sleep. Inflammation of the eyes was induced by too much work at night. A third affliction, eruption of carbuncles or boils, "was probably brought on by general physical debility to which the various features of Marx's style of life – alcohol, tobacco, poor diet, and failure to sleep – all contributed. Engels often exhorted Marx to alter this dangerous regime". In Seigel's thesis, what lay behind this punishing sacrifice of his health may have been guilt about self-involvement and egoism, originally induced in Karl Marx by his father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeigel1978495–496_198-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeigel1978495%E2%80%93496-198">[197]</a></sup>

In 2007, a [retrodiagnosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retrodiagnosis "Retrodiagnosis") of Marx's skin disease was made by [dermatologist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dermatologist "Dermatologist") Sam Shuster of [Newcastle University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newcastle_University "Newcastle University") and for Shuster, the most probable explanation was that Marx suffered not from liver problems, but from [hidradenitis suppurativa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hidradenitis_suppurativa "Hidradenitis suppurativa"), a recurring infective condition arising from blockage of [apocrine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apocrine "Apocrine") ducts opening into [hair follicles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hair_follicles "Hair follicles"). This condition, which was not described in the English medical literature until 1933 (hence would not have been known to Marx's physicians), can produce joint pain (which could be misdiagnosed as rheumatic disorder) and painful eye conditions. To arrive at his retrodiagnosis, Shuster considered the primary material: the Marx correspondence published in the 50 volumes of the _Marx/Engels Collected Works_. There, "although the skin lesions were called 'furuncles', 'boils' and 'carbuncles' by Marx, his wife, and his physicians, they were too persistent, recurrent, destructive and site-specific for that diagnosis". The sites of the persistent 'carbuncles' were noted repeatedly in the armpits, groins, [perianal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perianal "Perianal"), [genital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genital "Genital") ([penis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_penis "Human penis") and [scrotum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scrotum "Scrotum")) and [suprapubic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suprapubic "Suprapubic") regions and inner thighs, "favoured sites of hidradenitis suppurativa". Professor Shuster claimed the diagnosis "can now be made definitively".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShuster20081–2_199-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShuster20081%E2%80%932-199">[198]</a></sup>

Shuster went on to consider the potential [psychosocial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Psychosocial "Psychosocial") effects of the disease, noting that the skin is an organ of communication and that hidradenitis suppurativa produces much psychological distress, including loathing and disgust and depression of self-image, mood, and well-being, feelings for which Shuster found "much evidence" in the Marx correspondence. Professor Shuster went on to ask himself whether the mental effects of the disease affected Marx's work and even helped him to develop [his theory of alienation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation "Marx's theory of alienation").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShuster20083_200-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShuster20083-200">[199]</a></sup>

### Death

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg/220px-Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_(10).jpg)

[Tomb of Karl Marx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tomb_of_Karl_Marx "Tomb of Karl Marx"), East [Highgate Cemetery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highgate_Cemetery "Highgate Cemetery"), London

Following the death of his wife Jenny in December 1881, Marx developed a [catarrh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catarrh "Catarrh") that kept him in ill health for the last 15 months of his life. It eventually brought on the [bronchitis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronchitis "Bronchitis") and [pleurisy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleurisy "Pleurisy") that killed him in London on 14 March 1883, when he died a [stateless person](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statelessness "Statelessness") at age 64.<sup id="cite_ref-stateless_201-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-stateless-201">[200]</a></sup> Family and friends in London buried his body in [Highgate Cemetery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highgate_Cemetery "Highgate Cemetery") (East), London, on 17 March 1883 in an area reserved for agnostics and atheists ([George Eliot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Eliot "George Eliot")'s grave is nearby). According to [Francis Wheen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Wheen "Francis Wheen"), there were between nine and eleven mourners at his funeral;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheen2001[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3KOyuSakn80CpgPA382_382]_202-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheen2001[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3KOyuSakn80CpgPA382_382]-202">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup> however, research from contemporary sources identifies thirteen named individuals attending the funeral: [Friedrich Engels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Engels "Friedrich Engels"), [Eleanor Marx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eleanor_Marx "Eleanor Marx"), [Edward Aveling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Aveling "Edward Aveling"), [Paul Lafargue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Lafargue "Paul Lafargue"), [Charles Longuet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Longuet "Charles Longuet"), [Helene Demuth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helene_Demuth "Helene Demuth"), [Wilhelm Liebknecht](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Liebknecht "Wilhelm Liebknecht"), Gottlieb Lemke, [Frederick Lessner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_Lessner "Frederick Lessner"), G Lochner, Sir [Ray Lankester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Lankester "Ray Lankester"), [Carl Schorlemmer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Schorlemmer "Carl Schorlemmer") and [Ernest Radford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernest_Radford "Ernest Radford").<sup id="cite_ref-204"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup> A contemporary newspaper account claims that twenty-five to thirty relatives and friends attended the funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-205"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> A writer in _[The Graphic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Graphic "The Graphic")_ noted: 'By a strange blunder ... his death was not announced for two days, and then as having taken place at Paris. The next day the correction came from Paris; and when his friends and followers hastened to his house in [Haverstock Hill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haverstock_Hill "Haverstock Hill"), to learn the time and place of burial, they learned that he was already in the cold ground. But for this secresy \[sic\] and haste, a great popular demonstration would undoubtedly have been held over his grave'.<sup id="cite_ref-206"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup>

Several of his closest friends spoke at his funeral, including [Wilhelm Liebknecht](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Liebknecht "Wilhelm Liebknecht") and Friedrich Engels. Engels' speech included the passage:

> On the 14th of March, at a quarter to three in the afternoon, the greatest living thinker ceased to think. He had been left alone for scarcely two minutes, and when we came back we found him in his armchair, peacefully gone to sleep – but forever.<sup id="cite_ref-1883:_The_death_of_Karl_Marx_207-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-1883:_The_death_of_Karl_Marx-207">[206]</a></sup>

Marx's surviving daughters [Eleanor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eleanor_Marx "Eleanor Marx") and [Laura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laura_Marx "Laura Marx"), as well as [Charles Longuet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Longuet "Charles Longuet") and [Paul Lafargue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Lafargue "Paul Lafargue"), Marx's two French socialist sons-in-law, were also in attendance.<sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup> He had been predeceased by his wife and his eldest daughter, the latter dying a few months earlier in January 1883. Liebknecht, a founder and leader of the German Social Democratic Party, gave a speech in German, and Longuet, a prominent figure in the French working-class movement, made a short statement in French.<sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup> Two [telegrams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telegram "Telegram") from workers' parties in France and Spain<sup>[<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2021)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> were also read out.<sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup> Together with Engels's speech, this constituted the entire programme of the funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup> Non-relatives attending the funeral included three communist associates of Marx: Friedrich Lessner, imprisoned for three years after the [Cologne Communist Trial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cologne_Communist_Trial "Cologne Communist Trial") of 1852; G. Lochner, whom Engels described as "an old member of the Communist League"; and [Carl Schorlemmer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Schorlemmer "Carl Schorlemmer"), a professor of chemistry in Manchester, a member of the [Royal Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society"), and a communist activist involved in the 1848 [Baden revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolution_in_Baden "Revolution in Baden").<sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup> Another attendee of the funeral was [Ray Lankester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Lankester "Ray Lankester"), a British zoologist who would later become a prominent academic.<sup id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_203-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-203">[202]</a></sup>

Marx left a personal estate valued for probate at £250<sup id="cite_ref-probate_208-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-probate-208">[207]</a></sup> (equivalent to £38,095 in 2024).<sup id="cite_ref-209"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> Upon his own death in 1895, Engels left Marx's two surviving daughters a "significant portion" of his considerable estate (valued in 2024 at US$6.8 million).<sup id="cite_ref-Montefiore_190-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Montefiore-190">[189]</a></sup>

Marx and his family were reburied on a new site nearby in November 1954. The [tomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tomb_of_Karl_Marx "Tomb of Karl Marx") at the new site, unveiled on 14 March 1956,<sup id="cite_ref-auto_210-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-auto-210">[209]</a></sup> bears the carved message: "[Workers of All Lands Unite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workers_of_the_world,_unite! "Workers of the world, unite!")", the final line of _The Communist Manifesto_; and, from the 11th "[Thesis on Feuerbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theses_on_Feuerbach "Theses on Feuerbach")" (as edited by Engels), "The philosophers have only interpreted the world in various ways—the point however is to change it".<sup id="cite_ref-wh1_211-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-wh1-211">[210]</a></sup> The [Communist Party of Great Britain (CPGB)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain "Communist Party of Great Britain") had the monument with a portrait bust by [Laurence Bradshaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laurence_Bradshaw "Laurence Bradshaw") erected and Marx's original tomb had only humble adornment.<sup id="cite_ref-wh1_211-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-wh1-211">[210]</a></sup> Black civil rights leader and CPGB activist [Claudia Jones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claudia_Jones "Claudia Jones") was later buried beside Karl Marx's tomb.

The Marxist historian [Eric Hobsbawm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eric_Hobsbawm "Eric Hobsbawm") remarked: "One cannot say Marx died a failure." Although he had not achieved a large following of disciples in Britain, his writings had already begun to make an impact on the left-wing movements in Germany and Russia. Within twenty-five years of his death, the continental European socialist parties that acknowledged Marx's influence on their politics had contributed to significant gains in their [representative democratic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representative_democracy "Representative democracy") elections.<sup id="cite_ref-212"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup>

## Thought

### Influences

Marx's thought demonstrates influence from many sources, including but not limited to:

-   [Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel "Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel")'s philosophy<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup>
-   The classical political economy (economics) of Adam Smith and [David Ricardo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Ricardo "David Ricardo"),<sup id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_214-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Sherman1995-214">[213]</a></sup> as well as [Jean Charles Léonard de Sismondi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Charles_L%C3%A9onard_de_Sismondi "Jean Charles Léonard de Sismondi")'s critique of laissez-faire economics and analysis of the precarious state of the proletariat<sup id="cite_ref-Paresh_215-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Paresh-215">[214]</a></sup>
-   [French socialist thought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_Left_in_France "History of the Left in France"),<sup id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_214-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Sherman1995-214">[213]</a></sup> in particular the thought of [Jean-Jacques Rousseau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau "Jean-Jacques Rousseau"), [Henri de Saint-Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henri_de_Saint-Simon "Henri de Saint-Simon"), [Pierre-Joseph Proudhon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon "Pierre-Joseph Proudhon") and [Charles Fourier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Fourier "Charles Fourier")<sup id="cite_ref-Beilharz1992_216-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Beilharz1992-216">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup>
-   Earlier German philosophical materialism among the [Young Hegelians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young_Hegelians "Young Hegelians"), particularly that of [Ludwig Feuerbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach "Ludwig Feuerbach") and [Bruno Bauer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bruno_Bauer "Bruno Bauer"),<sup id="cite_ref-et_71-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-et-71">[70]</a></sup> as well as the French materialism of the late 18th century, including [Diderot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diderot "Diderot"), [Claude Adrien Helvétius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claude_Adrien_Helv%C3%A9tius "Claude Adrien Helvétius") and [d'Holbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%27Holbach "D'Holbach")
-   [Friedrich Engels'](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Engels "Friedrich Engels") analysis of the working class,<sup id="cite_ref-Bottomore1991_67-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Bottomore1991-67">[66]</a></sup> as well as the early descriptions of class provided by French liberals and Saint-Simonians such as [François Guizot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Guizot "François Guizot") and [Augustin Thierry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augustin_Thierry "Augustin Thierry")
-   Marx's Judaic legacy has been identified as formative to both his moral outlook<sup id="cite_ref-218"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup> and his materialist philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeigel1978112–119_219-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeigel1978112%E2%80%93119-219">[218]</a></sup>

Marx's view of history, which came to be called [historical materialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_materialism "Historical materialism") (controversially adapted as the philosophy of [dialectical materialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dialectical_materialism "Dialectical materialism") by Engels and Lenin), certainly shows the influence of Hegel's claim that one should view reality (and history) [dialectically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dialectic "Dialectic").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> However, whereas Hegel had thought in [idealist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idealist "Idealist") terms, putting ideas in the forefront, Marx sought to conceptualise dialectics in [materialist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materialist "Materialist") terms, arguing for the primacy of matter over idea.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> Where Hegel saw the "spirit" as driving history, Marx saw this as an unnecessary mystification, obscuring the reality of humanity and its physical actions shaping the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> He wrote that Hegelianism stood the movement of reality on its head, and that one needed to set it upon its feet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> Despite his dislike of mystical terms, Marx used [Gothic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gothic_fiction "Gothic fiction") language in several of his works: in _The Communist Manifesto_ he proclaims "A spectre is haunting Europe – the spectre of communism. All the powers of old Europe have entered into a holy alliance to exorcise this spectre", and in _The Capital_ he refers to capital as "[necromancy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necromancy "Necromancy") that surrounds the products of labour".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_220-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-autogenerated2-220">[219]</a></sup>

Though inspired by French socialist and sociological thought,<sup id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_214-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Sherman1995-214">[213]</a></sup> Marx criticised [utopian socialists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utopian_socialists "Utopian socialists"), arguing that their favoured small-scale socialistic communities would be bound to marginalisation and poverty and that only a large-scale change in the [economic system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_system "Economic system") could bring about real change.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup>

Other important contributions to Marx's revision of Hegelianism came from Engels's book, _[The Condition of the Working Class in England in 1844](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Condition_of_the_Working_Class_in_England_in_1844 "The Condition of the Working Class in England in 1844")_, which led Marx to conceive of the historical dialectic in terms of [class conflict](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_conflict "Class conflict") and to see the modern working class as the most progressive force for revolution,<sup id="cite_ref-Bottomore1991_67-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Bottomore1991-67">[66]</a></sup> as well as from the social democrat [Friedrich Wilhelm Schulz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Schulz "Friedrich Wilhelm Schulz"), who in _Die Bewegung der Produktion_ described the movement of society as "flowing from the contradiction between the forces of production and the mode of production."<sup id="cite_ref-Levine_221-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Levine-221">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated144_222-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-autogenerated144-222">[221]</a></sup>

Marx believed that he could study history and society scientifically, discerning tendencies of history and thereby predicting the outcome of social conflicts. Some followers of Marx, therefore, concluded that a communist revolution would inevitably occur. However, Marx famously asserted in the eleventh of his "[Theses on Feuerbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theses_on_Feuerbach "Theses on Feuerbach")" that "philosophers have only interpreted the world, in various ways; the point however is to change it" and he clearly dedicated himself to trying to alter the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223–24_7-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223%E2%80%9324-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wh1_211-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-wh1-211">[210]</a></sup>

Marx's theories inspired several theories and disciplines of future, including but not limited to:

-   [Contemporary critique of political economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy "Critique of political economy")
-   [Kondratiev wave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kondratiev_wave "Kondratiev wave") and [Kuznets swing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuznets_swing "Kuznets swing")
-   Theory of [Underconsumption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Underconsumption "Underconsumption")
-   [Creative destruction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creative_destruction "Creative destruction")
-   [Crisis theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crisis_theory "Crisis theory")
-   [Quantitative Economic History](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantitative_Economic_History "Quantitative Economic History")
-   [World-systems theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World-systems_theory "World-systems theory")

### Philosophy and social thought

Marx's polemic with other thinkers often occurred through [critique](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critique "Critique") and thus he has been called "the first great user of [critical method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critical_method "Critical method") in social sciences".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_214-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Sherman1995-214">[213]</a></sup> He criticised speculative philosophy, equating [metaphysics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metaphysics "Metaphysics") with ideology. By adopting this approach, Marx attempted to separate key findings from ideological [biases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bias "Bias").<sup id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_214-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Sherman1995-214">[213]</a></sup> This set him apart from many contemporary philosophers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223–24_7-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223%E2%80%9324-7">[6]</a></sup>

#### Human nature

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg/192px-G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:G.W.F._Hegel_(by_Sichling,_after_Sebbers).jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/192px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg)

The philosophers G.W.F. Hegel (left) and [Ludwig Feuerbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach "Ludwig Feuerbach"), whose ideas on dialectics heavily influenced Marx

Like Tocqueville, who described a faceless and bureaucratic [despotism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Despotism "Despotism") with no identifiable despot,<sup id="cite_ref-224"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup> Marx also broke with classical thinkers who spoke of a single tyrant and with [Montesquieu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montesquieu "Montesquieu"), who discussed the nature of the single despot. Instead, Marx set out to analyse "the despotism of capital".<sup id="cite_ref-225"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup> Fundamentally, Marx assumed that [human history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_history "Human history") involves transforming [human nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_nature "Human nature"), which encompasses both human beings and material objects.<sup id="cite_ref-Ollman1973_226-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Ollman1973-226">[225]</a></sup> Humans recognise that they possess both actual and potential selves.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_labour_227-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_labour-227">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marx_critique_228-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_critique-228">[227]</a></sup> For both Marx and Hegel, self-development begins with an experience of internal [alienation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation "Marx's theory of alienation") stemming from this recognition, followed by a realisation that the actual self, as a [subjective](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subject_(philosophy) "Subject (philosophy)") agent, renders its potential counterpart an [object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Object_(philosophy) "Object (philosophy)") to be apprehended.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_critique_228-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_critique-228">[227]</a></sup> Marx further argues that by moulding nature<sup id="cite_ref-Lefever_229-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Lefever-229">[228]</a></sup> in desired ways<sup id="cite_ref-Holland_desire_230-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Holland_desire-230">[229]</a></sup> the subject takes the object as its own and thus permits the individual to be actualised as fully human. For Marx, the [human nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_human_nature "Marx's theory of human nature") – _Gattungswesen_, or [species-being](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species-being "Species-being") – exists as a function of human labour.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_labour_227-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_labour-227">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marx_critique_228-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_critique-228">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holland_desire_230-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Holland_desire-230">[229]</a></sup> Fundamental to Marx's idea of meaningful labour is the proposition that for a subject to come to terms with its alienated object it must first exert influence upon literal, [material objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materialism "Materialism") in the subject's world.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_objects_231-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_objects-231">[230]</a></sup> Marx acknowledges that Hegel "grasps the nature of work and comprehends objective man, authentic because actual, as the result of his _own work_",<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_work_232-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_work-232">[231]</a></sup> but characterises Hegelian self-development as unduly "spiritual" and abstract.<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_spiritual_233-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_spiritual-233">[232]</a></sup> Marx thus departs from Hegel by insisting that "the fact that man is a corporeal, actual, sentient, objective being with natural capacities means that he has actual, sensuous objects for his nature as objects of his life-expression, or that he can only express his life in actual sensuous objects".<sup id="cite_ref-Marx_objects_231-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Marx_objects-231">[230]</a></sup> Consequently, Marx revises Hegelian "work" into material "[labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labour_(economics) "Labour (economics)")" and in the context of human capacity to transform nature the term "[labour power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labour_power "Labour power")".<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup>

#### Labour, class struggle and false consciousness

> The history of all hitherto existing society is the history of class struggles.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg/170px-Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg)

A monument dedicated to Marx and Engels in [Shanghai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shanghai "Shanghai"), China

Marx had a special concern with how people relate to their own labour power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222_235-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222-235">[234]</a></sup> He wrote extensively about this in terms of the problem of [alienation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation "Marx's theory of alienation").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMészáros200696_236-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM%C3%A9sz%C3%A1ros200696-236">[235]</a></sup> As with the dialectic, Marx began with a Hegelian notion of alienation but developed a more materialist conception.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222_235-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222-235">[234]</a></sup> [Capitalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitalism "Capitalism") mediates social relationships of production (such as among workers or between workers and capitalists) through commodities, including labour, that are bought and sold on the market.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222_235-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222-235">[234]</a></sup> For Marx, the possibility that one may give up ownership of one's own labour – one's capacity to transform the world – is tantamount to being alienated from one's own nature and it is a spiritual loss.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222_235-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222-235">[234]</a></sup> Marx described this loss as [commodity fetishism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodity_fetishism "Commodity fetishism"), in which the things that people produce, commodities, appear to have a life and movement of their own to which humans and their behaviour merely adapt.<sup id="cite_ref-Balibar1995_237-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Balibar1995-237">[236]</a></sup>

[Commodity fetishism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commodity_fetishism "Commodity fetishism") provides an example of what Engels called "[false consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/False_consciousness "False consciousness")",<sup id="cite_ref-KołakowskiFalla2005_238-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Ko%C5%82akowskiFalla2005-238">[237]</a></sup> which relates closely to the understanding of ideology. By "ideology", Marx and Engels meant ideas that reflect the interests of a particular class at a particular time in history, but which contemporaries see as universal and eternal.<sup id="cite_ref-Hernadi1989_239-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Hernadi1989-239">[238]</a></sup> Marx and Engels's point was not only that such beliefs are at best half-truths, as they serve an important political function. Put another way, the control that one class exercises over the means of production include not only the production of food or manufactured goods but also the production of ideas (this provides one possible explanation for why members of a subordinate class may hold ideas contrary to their own interests).<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thompson1990_240-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Thompson1990-240">[239]</a></sup> An example of this sort of analysis is Marx's understanding of religion, summed up in a passage from the preface<sup id="cite_ref-241"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup> to his 1843 _Contribution to the [Critique of Hegel's Philosophy of Right](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critique_of_Hegel%27s_Philosophy_of_Right "Critique of Hegel's Philosophy of Right")_:

> Religious suffering is, at one and the same time, the expression of real suffering and a protest against real suffering. Religion is the sigh of the oppressed creature, the heart of a heartless world, and the soul of soulless conditions. It is the opium of the people. The abolition of religion as the illusory happiness of the people is the demand for their real happiness. To call on them to give up their illusions about their condition is to call on them to give up a condition that requires illusions.<sup id="cite_ref-MarxO'Malley1977_242-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-MarxO'Malley1977-242">[241]</a></sup>

Whereas his [Gymnasium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gymnasium_(school) "Gymnasium (school)") senior thesis at the [Gymnasium zu Trier](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium_(Trier)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium (Trier) (page does not exist)") \[[de](https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium_(Trier) "de:Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium (Trier)")\] argued that religion had as its primary social aim the promotion of [solidarity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solidarity_(sociology) "Solidarity (sociology)"), here Marx sees the social function of religion in terms of highlighting/preserving political and economic _status quo_ and [inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_inequality "Economic inequality").<sup id="cite_ref-SwatosKivisto1998_243-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-SwatosKivisto1998-243">[242]</a></sup>

Marx was an outspoken opponent of [child labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Child_labour "Child labour"),<sup id="cite_ref-244"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-244">[243]</a></sup> saying that British industries "could but live by sucking blood, and children's blood too", and that U.S. capital was financed by the "capitalized blood of children".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_220-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-autogenerated2-220">[219]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-245">[244]</a></sup>

#### Critique of political economy, history and society

> But you Communists would introduce community of women, screams the whole bourgeoisie in chorus. The bourgeois sees in his wife a mere instrument of production. He hears that the means of production are to be exploited in common, and, naturally, can come to no other conclusion than that the lot of being common to all will likewise fall to the women. He has not even a suspicion that the real point aimed at is to do away with the status of women as mere mean of production.

—Karl Marx, _The Communist Manifesto_<sup id="cite_ref-246"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup>

Marx's thoughts on labour and its function in reproducing capital were related to the primacy he gave to social relations in determining the society's past, present and future.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Turner2005_247-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Turner2005-247">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_248-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-:1-248">[247]</a></sup> (Critics have called this [economic determinism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_determinism "Economic determinism").) Labour is the precondition for the existence of, and [accumulation of capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accumulation_of_capital "Accumulation of capital"), which both shape the [social system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_system "Social system").<sup id="cite_ref-:1_248-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-:1-248">[247]</a></sup> For Marx, [social change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_change "Social change") was driven by conflict between opposing interests, by parties situated in the historical situation of their [mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_of_production "Mode of production").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun2012_175-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun2012-175">[174]</a></sup> This became the inspiration for the body of works known as the [conflict theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conflict_theory "Conflict theory").<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2005_247-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Turner2005-247">[246]</a></sup> In his [evolutionary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sociocultural_evolution "Sociocultural evolution") model of history, he argued that [human history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_history "Human history") began with free, productive and creative activities that was over time coerced and dehumanised, a trend most apparent under capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> Marx noted that this was not an intentional process, but rather due to the immanent logic of the current [mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_of_production "Mode of production") which demands more human labour ([abstract labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_labour_and_concrete_labour "Abstract labour and concrete labour")) to reproduce the social relationships of capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPostone2006190,_26–27._135,_374–75_174-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPostone2006190,_26%E2%80%9327._135,_374%E2%80%9375-174">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPepperell2010104–105_176-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPepperell2010104%E2%80%93105-176">[175]</a></sup>

The organisation of society depends on [means of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Means_of_production "Means of production"). The means of production are all things required to produce material goods, such as land, natural resources, and technology but not human labour. The [relations of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relations_of_production "Relations of production") are the social relationships people enter into as they acquire and use the means of production.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2005_247-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Turner2005-247">[246]</a></sup> Together, these compose the [mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_of_production "Mode of production") and Marx distinguished historical eras in terms of modes of production. Marx differentiated between [base and superstructure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_and_superstructure "Base and superstructure"), where the base (or substructure) is the [economic system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_system "Economic system") and superstructure is the cultural and political system.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2005_247-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Turner2005-247">[246]</a></sup> Marx regarded this mismatch between economic base and social [superstructure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_and_superstructure "Base and superstructure") as a major source of social disruption and conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2005_247-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Turner2005-247">[246]</a></sup>

Despite Marx's stress on the critique of capitalism and discussion of the new [communist society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_society "Communist society") that should replace it, his explicit critique is guarded, as he saw it as an improved society compared to the past ones ([slavery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slavery "Slavery") and [feudalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feudalism "Feudalism")).<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-8"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup> Marx never clearly discusses issues of [morality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morality "Morality") and [justice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Justice "Justice"), but scholars agree that his work contained [implicit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logical_implication "Logical implication") discussion of those concepts.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-9"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg/220px-Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg)

Marx's view of capitalism was two-sided.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-10"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wood1993_152-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Wood1993-152">[151]</a></sup> On one hand, in the 19th century's deepest critique of the dehumanising aspects of this system he noted that defining features of capitalism include alienation, exploitation and recurring, cyclical [depressions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_depressions "Economic depressions") leading to mass unemployment. On the other hand, he characterised capitalism as "revolutionising, industrialising and universalising qualities of development, growth and progressivity" (by which Marx meant industrialisation, urbanisation, [technological progress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technological_progress "Technological progress"), increased [productivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Productivity "Productivity") and growth, [rationality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rationality "Rationality"), and [scientific revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_revolution "Scientific revolution")) that are responsible for progress, at in contrast to earlier forms of societies.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-11"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wood1993_152-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Wood1993-152">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-8"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> Marx considered the capitalist class to be one of the most revolutionary in history because it constantly improved the means of production, more so than any other class in history and was responsible for the overthrow of [feudalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feudalism "Feudalism").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gilbert2010_249-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Gilbert2010-249">[248]</a></sup> Capitalism can stimulate considerable growth because the capitalist has an incentive to reinvest profits in new technologies and [capital equipment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capital_equipment "Capital equipment").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222_235-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200222-235">[234]</a></sup>

According to Marx, capitalists take advantage of the difference between the labour market and the market for whatever commodity the capitalist can produce. Marx observed that in practically every successful industry, input unit-costs are lower than output unit-prices. Marx called the difference "[surplus value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surplus_value "Surplus value")" and argued that it was based on [surplus labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surplus_labour "Surplus labour"), the difference between what it costs to keep workers alive, and what they can produce.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-12"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup> Although Marx describes capitalists as [vampires](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vampire "Vampire") sucking worker's blood,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-9"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> he notes that drawing profit is "by no means an injustice" since Marx, according to [Allen W. Wood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_W._Wood "Allen W. Wood") "excludes any trans-epochal standpoint from which one can comment" on the morals of such particular arrangements.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-13"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup> Marx also noted that even the capitalists themselves cannot go against the system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup> The problem is the "cancerous cell" of [capital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capital_(economics) "Capital (economics)"), understood not as property or equipment, but the social relations between workers and owners, (the selling and purchasing of labour power) – the societal system, or rather [mode of production](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_of_production "Mode of production"), in general.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup>

At the same time, Marx stressed that capitalism was unstable and prone to [periodic crises](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crisis_theory "Crisis theory").<sup id="cite_ref-wk_95-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-wk-95">[94]</a></sup> He suggested that over time capitalists would invest more and more in new technologies and less and less in labour.<sup id="cite_ref-sep_81-14"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-sep-81">[80]</a></sup> Since Marx believed that profit derived from surplus value appropriated from labour, he concluded that the rate of profit would fall as the economy grows.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223_250-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223-250">[249]</a></sup> Marx believed that increasingly severe crises would punctuate this [cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Business_cycles "Business cycles") of growth and collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223_250-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223-250">[249]</a></sup> Moreover, he believed that in the long-term, this process would enrich and [empower](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empowerment "Empowerment") the capitalist class and impoverish the proletariat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223_250-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223-250">[249]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-5"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup> In section one of _The Communist Manifesto_, Marx describes [feudalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feudalism "Feudalism"), capitalism and the role internal social contradictions play in the historical process:

> We see then: the means of production and of exchange, on whose foundation the bourgeoisie built itself up, were generated in feudal society. At a certain stage in the development of these means of production and of exchange, the conditions under which feudal society produced and exchanged ... the feudal relations of property became no longer compatible with the already developed productive forces; they became so many fetters. They had to be burst asunder; they were burst asunder. Into their place stepped free competition, accompanied by a social and political constitution adapted in it, and the economic and political sway of the bourgeois class. A similar movement is going on before our own eyes ... The productive forces at the disposal of society no longer tend to further the development of the conditions of bourgeois property; on the contrary, they have become too powerful for these conditions, by which they are fettered, and so soon as they overcome these fetters, they bring order into the whole of bourgeois society, endanger the existence of bourgeois property.<sup id="cite_ref-manifesto_5-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-manifesto-5">[4]</a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg/220px-MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg)

Outside a factory in [Oldham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldham "Oldham"). Marx believed that industrial workers (the [proletariat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proletariat "Proletariat")) would rise up around the world.

Marx believed that those structural contradictions within capitalism necessitate its end, giving way to socialism, or a [post-capitalistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-capitalism "Post-capitalism"), communist society:

> The development of modern industry, therefore, cuts from under its feet the very foundation on which the bourgeoisie produces and appropriates products. What the bourgeoisie, therefore, produces, above all, are its own grave-diggers. Its fall and the victory of the proletariat are equally inevitable.<sup id="cite_ref-manifesto_5-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-manifesto-5">[4]</a></sup>

Thanks to various processes overseen by capitalism, such as urbanisation, the working class, the proletariat, should grow in numbers and develop [class consciousness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_consciousness "Class consciousness"), in time realising that they can and must change the system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-10"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> Marx believed that if the proletariat were to seize the means of production, they would encourage social relations that would benefit everyone equally, abolishing the exploiting class and introducing a system of production less vulnerable to cyclical crises.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220–23_213-11"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200220%E2%80%9323-213">[212]</a></sup> Marx argued in _[The German Ideology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_German_Ideology "The German Ideology")_ that capitalism will end through the organised actions of an international working class:

> Communism is for us not a state of affairs which is to be established, an ideal to which reality will have to adjust itself. We call communism the real movement which abolishes the present state of things. The conditions of this movement result from the premises now in existence.<sup id="cite_ref-Elster1985_251-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Elster1985-251">[250]</a></sup>

In this new society, the alienation would end and humans would be free to act without being bound by selling their labour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223_250-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223-250">[249]</a></sup> It would be a democratic society, enfranchising the entire population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-6"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup> In such a [utopian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utopia "Utopia") world, there would also be little need for a state, whose goal was previously to enforce the alienation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223_250-4"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalhoun200223-250">[249]</a></sup> Marx theorised that between capitalism and the establishment of a socialist/communist system, would exist a period of [dictatorship of the proletariat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat "Dictatorship of the proletariat") – where the working class holds political power and forcibly socialises the means of production.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClark199857–59_217-7"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClark199857%E2%80%9359-217">[216]</a></sup> As he wrote in his _[Critique of the Gotha Program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critique_of_the_Gotha_Program "Critique of the Gotha Program")_, "between capitalist and communist society there lies the period of the [revolutionary transformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Communist_revolution "Communist revolution") of the one into the other. Corresponding to this is also a political transition period in which the state can be nothing but the revolutionary dictatorship of the proletariat".<sup id="cite_ref-Karl_Marx:Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme_252-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-Karl_Marx:Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme-252">[251]</a></sup> While he allowed for the possibility of peaceful transition in some countries with strong [democratic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democracy "Democracy") institutional structures (such as [Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom"), the [United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States "United States"), and the [Netherlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netherlands "Netherlands")), he suggested that in other countries in which workers cannot "attain their goal by peaceful means" the "lever of our revolution must be force".<sup id="cite_ref-253"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx#cite_note-253">[252]</a></sup>

### International relations

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg/220px-Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg)

[Karl Marx Monument](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Marx_Monument "Karl Marx Monument") in [Chemnitz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemnitz "Chemnitz") (known as _Karl-Marx-Stadt_ from 1953 to 1990)
